tf.Tensor(b'Item\xc2\xa07.\n    Management\xe2\x80\x99s Discussion and Analysis of Financial Condition and Results of Operations. \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nThe following discussion and analysis provides information\nthat we believe is relevant to an assessment and understanding of our results of operation and financial condition. You should\nread this analysis in conjunction with our audited consolidated financial statements and related footnotes. This discussion and\nanalysis contains statements of a forward-looking nature relating to future events or our future financial performance. These statements\ninvolve known and unknown risks, uncertainties and other factors that may cause actual results, level of activity, performance\nor achievements to be materially different from any future results, levels of activity, performance or achievements expressed or\nimplied by these forward-looking statements, including those set forth in this Form 10-K. \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n\n    29\n    \xc2\xa0\n    \n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\nOverview\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe are a residential and small business commercial solar energy\nengineering, procurement and construction firm. We offer turnkey services, including design, procurement, permitting, build-out,\ngrid connection, financing referrals and warranty and customer satisfaction activities. Our solar energy systems use high-quality\nsolar photovoltaic modules. We use proven technologies and techniques to help customers achieve meaningful savings by reducing\ntheir utility costs. In addition, we help customers lower their reliance upon fossil fuel energy sources. As of September 29, 2017,\nwe are the exclusive domestic and international licensee of POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 in-roof solar shingle, an innovative and aesthetically\npleasing solar shingle system developed by Dow.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe, including our predecessors, have more than 39 years of experience\nin residential solar energy and trace our roots to 1978, when Real Goods Trading Corporation sold the first solar photovoltaic\npanels in the United States. We have designed and installed over 26,000 residential and commercial solar energy systems since our\nfounding.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nDuring 2014, we discontinued our entire former Commercial segment\nand sold the assets associated with our catalog business (a portion of the Other segment). As of September 30, 2017, we created\na new segment for our POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 business. As a result, we now operate as four reportable segments: (1)\xc2\xa0Residential\n\xe2\x80\x93 the installation of solar energy systems for homeowners, including lease financing thereof, and small business commercial\nin the continental United States; (2)\xc2\xa0Sunetric \xe2\x80\x93 the installation of solar energy systems for both homeowners and business\nowners (commercial) in Hawaii; (3) POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 - the manufacturing and sales of solar shingles; and (4)\xc2\xa0Other \xe2\x80\x93\ncorporate operations. We believe this structure enables us to more effectively manage our operations and resources.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe generally recognize revenue from solar energy systems sold\nto our customers when we install the solar energy system. Our business requires that we incur costs of acquiring solar panels and\nlabor to install solar energy systems on our customer rooftops up-front and receive cash from customers thereafter. As a result,\nduring periods when we are increasing sales, we expect to have negative cash flow from operations.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nPOWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 License Agreement\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nA material significant event occurred on September 29, 2017\n(the \xe2\x80\x9cEffective Date\xe2\x80\x9d), when we executed the License with Dow, providing an exclusive domestic and international right to commercialize its POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 in-roof solar shingle,\nan innovative and aesthetically pleasing solar shingle system developed by Dow. The POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 1.0 and 2.0 versions used\nCIGS (copper indium gallium selenide solar cells) technology which had a high manufacturing cost, resulting in the product not\nbeing consumer price friendly. Conversely, the POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 3.0 is being developed with traditional silicon solar cells to\nincrease solar production and to provide a competitive consumer price point. The License requires us to commercialize and sell\na minimum of 50 megawatts of solar within 5-years of the Effective Date to retain exclusive world-wide rights, a requirement we\nbelieve is achievable.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nIn addition to the License, we executed a Trademark License Agreement\n(the \xe2\x80\x9cTLA\xe2\x80\x9d), a Technology Service Agreement (the \xe2\x80\x9cTSA\xe2\x80\x9d) and a Sales Agreement-Surplus Property (the \xe2\x80\x9cSales\nAgreement\xe2\x80\x9d) with Dow. The execution of the TLA will allow us to market the POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 3.0 product using the Dow name.\nThe Sales Agreement identified used manufacturing molds that Dow transferred title to us for a cost of $1.00.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\nUnder the terms of the License, we will produce, market and\nsell POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 3.0, for which we have agreed to a license fee of $3 million and a running royalty fee equal to 2.5% against\nnet sales of the POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 product and services, payable quarterly in arrears. Further, we will be responsible for all\npost-Effective Date costs to obtain UL Certification and for the prosecution\nof all related patents world-wide, which may be offset against the payment of the running royalty fee. The license fee is comprised\nof two payments; the first $1 million was paid within 10 days of the Effective Date of the License in 2017 in accordance with the\nTLA. The remaining $2 million is due within 30 days of the Company receiving UL Certification of the POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 3.0 product.\nUpon obtaining UL Certification, we intend to begin commercialization of POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 3.0 entailing the manufacturing, marketing\nand sale of POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 3.0 to roofing companies.\n\n\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nAs of December 31, 2017, we have invested approximately $1.1 million\nthat has been capitalized to the POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 License, an intangible asset on the Consolidated Balance Sheet.\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n\n    30\n    \xc2\xa0\n    \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nOther Key Metrics\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nBacklog\n\nBacklog is discussed below and an important\nmetric as we implement our revenue growth strategy.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nKey Operational Metric, Gross Margin\non Residential Segment, Our Largest Segment \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe utilize a job costing system whereby\nemployees record their time to projects. We accumulate the cost of idle time reflecting the cost we incur to maintain a construction\norganization until our revenue grows, allowing for greater utilization of our construction organization. Cost of goods sold (\xe2\x80\x9cCOGS\xe2\x80\x9d)\ninclude direct project installation costs (materials, labor, travel, financing fees, and estimated warranty costs) and indirect\ncosts for project installation support (including un-utilized labor of idle time of construction crews, supplies, and insurance).\nWe employ an internal time reporting system to determine COGS and resulting gross margin percentage used by the company to measure\nits performance in achieving gross margin percentage targets. Further, we measure COGS per watt based upon COGS, excluding idle\ntime, divided by the aggregate watts of systems installed during the period. For financial reporting purposes, COGS include the\nidle time of construction crews currently maintained by the company in anticipation of future growth of backlog. Gross margin percentage\non actual installation time is not a measure defined by generally accepted accounting principles.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nFor the year ended December 31, 2017, as a result of the competitive\nlandscape, the average selling price per watt (\xe2\x80\x9cASP\xe2\x80\x9d) declined at a greater rate than the cost of installation per\nwatt. Accordingly, our ASP declined 10% resulting in a roughly 6% and 8% decline in gross margin based on actual installation\ntime and including idle time, respectively. We anticipate an improvement in our gross margin percentage in future periods from\nincreased revenue from our implementation of our revenue growth strategy.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n    \xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n    Twelve Months Ended \nDecember 31, 2017\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n    Twelve Months Ended \nDecember 31, 2016\xc2\xa0\n\n    Gross margin % on actual installation time\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa024%\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa030%\n\n    Gross margin % including idle time\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa013%\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa021%\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nBacklog \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nBacklog represents the dollar amount of revenue that may be\nrecognized in the future from signed contracts to install solar energy systems that have not yet been installed without taking\ninto account possible future cancellations. Backlog is not a measure defined by generally accepted accounting principles, and\nis not a measure of contract profitability. Our methodology for determining backlog may not be comparable to methodologies used\nby other companies in determining their backlog amounts. The backlog amounts we disclose are net of cancellations received and\ninclude anticipated revenues associated with (i) the original contract amounts, and (ii) change orders for which we have received\nwritten confirmations from customers. Backlog may not be indicative of future operating results, and projects in our backlog may\nbe cancelled, modified or otherwise altered by customers.\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nThe following table summarizes changes to our backlog by segment\nfor the years ended December 31, 2016 and December 31, 2017:\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n    (in thousands)\xc2\xa0\n    Residential\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n solar industry wherein customers shop price after signing a contract and\nexercise their right to cancel during a three day rescission period after we countersign their contract, (ii) customer home\xe2\x80\x99s\nphysical condition requires upgrades that they may not be able to afford or reduces their return on investment, (iii) delays to\ninstalling their system within their desired timeframe, and (iv) our history of operating losses and resulting declining stock\nprice affecting customer decisions. We determined that for optimum internal operations, and customer satisfaction, that a backlog\nequivalent to a few months of sales is optimal.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe intend to continue the optimization of our e-sales call -center-\nbased sales organization with the goal of increasing our future sales awards, both for our current states of operation and new\nstates where we may operate in future periods. Our customers currently finance their acquisition of solar energy systems using\ntheir own cash, or a loan they receive from a financial provider. We believe that to be successful in increasing our sales and\nresultant revenue, we intend to do the following as part of our revenue growth strategy:\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xb7Invest in the POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 license by obtaining UL Certification for POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 3.0, a prerequisite for\n                                                                                                               commercialization of the product and, upon achieving UL Certification, manufacture, market and sell the POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 3.0\n                                                                                                               to roofing companies and new home builders;\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xb7Leverage the POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 brand to generate leads and revenue for the Solar Division;\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xb7Leverage our investment in RGS 365\xe2\x84\xa2 customer-centric software for the POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 and Solar Divisions;\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xb7Expand our digital marketing program to generate customer leads while achieving our desired cost of acquisition;\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xb7Make available to our customers, additional third-party providers to finance customer acquisitions of our solar energy systems;\nand\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xb7Expand our network of authorized third-party installers.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe compete with larger, better-financed firms for customers,\nemployees, and the services of third party financiers and installers and, accordingly, there can be no assurance that we will be\nsuccessful in meeting our goals for increasing sales and revenue.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nRecent Developments\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nIn conjunction with our plans to position our company for future\nprofitable operations, we have:\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n\n\xc2\xb7Entered into an agreement for an up to $10.75 million convertible note and warrant offering in March 2018 to raise approximately\nup to $9.1 million after costs. We will receive gross proceeds of $5.0 million at closing and may receive up to $5.0 million more\nin the future upon prepayment of the Investor Notes, if any. It is our intent to use the proceeds from the 2018 Offering to obtain\nUL Certification for POWERHOUSE \xe2\x84\xa2 which will then require a $2 million payment to Dow, and (ii) initiate commercialization\nof POWERHOUSE \xe2\x84\xa2.\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xb7Launched a realignment of the Solar Division to reduce fixed cost structure below the gross profit line by 30% as compared\nto 2017 costs to lower the required amount of future revenue to achieve break-even, or better, operating results in the future.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xb7Raised net proceeds of $1.8 million from offerings of Class A common stock and warrants in January 2018.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n\n    32\n    \xc2\xa0\n    \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\nCritical Accounting Policies and Estimates \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nOur discussion and analysis of our financial condition and results\nof operations are based on our financial statements, which have been prepared in accordance with U.S. generally accepted accounting\nprinciples, or \xe2\x80\x9cGAAP.\xe2\x80\x9d The preparation of these financial statements requires us to make estimates and judgments that\naffect the reported amounts of assets, liabilities, revenue and expenses. We base our estimates on historical experience and on\nvarious other assumptions that we believe to be reasonable under the circumstances, the results of which form the basis for making\njudgments about the carrying value of assets and liabilities that are not readily apparent from other sources. Actual results may\ndiffer from these estimates under different assumptions or conditions. We have identified the following to be critical accounting\npolicies whose application have a material impact on our reported results of operations, and which involve a higher degree of complexity,\nas they require us to make judgments and estimates about matters that are inherently uncertain.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nRevenue Recognition \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nFor sales of solar energy systems and components of less\nthan 100 kW, \xe2\x80\x9cresidential and small commercial customers\xe2\x80\x9d, we recognize revenue in accordance with ASC 605-25,\nRevenue Recognition\xe2\x80\x94Multiple-Element Arrangements, and ASC 605-10-S99, Revenue\nRecognition\xe2\x80\x94Overall\xe2\x80\x94SEC Materials. Revenue is recognized when (1)\xc2\xa0persuasive evidence of an arrangement\nexists, (2)\xc2\xa0delivery has occurred or services have been rendered, (3)\xc2\xa0the sales price is fixed or determinable, and\n(4)\xc2\xa0collection of the related receivable is reasonably assured. Components are principally comprised of photovoltaic\npanels, inverters, and solar energy system mounting hardware. We recognize revenue when we install a solar energy system,\nprovided all other revenue recognition criteria have been met. Costs incurred on residential installations before the solar\nenergy systems are completed are deferred and included in other current assets as work in progress in the consolidated\nbalance sheet.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nFor those systems of 100 kW or greater, \xe2\x80\x9ccommercial customers,\xe2\x80\x9d\nwe recognize revenue according to ASC 605-35, Revenue Recognition\xe2\x80\x94Construction-Type and Production Type Contracts.\nRevenue is recognized on a percentage-of-completion basis, based on the ratio of total costs incurred to date to total projected\ncosts. Provisions are made for the full amount of any anticipated losses on a contract-by-contract basis.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nThe assets \xe2\x80\x9cCosts in excess of billings\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cDeferred\ncosts on uncompleted contracts\xe2\x80\x9d represent costs incurred plus estimated earnings in excess of amounts billed on percentage-of-completion\nmethod contracts and costs incurred but deferred until recognition of the related contract revenue on completed-method contracts,\nrespectively. The liability \xe2\x80\x9cBillings in excess of costs on uncompleted contracts\xe2\x80\x9d represents billings in excess of\nrelated costs and earned profit on percentage-of-completion method contracts. We invoice commercial customers according to milestones\ndefined in their respective contracts. The prerequisite for billing is the completion of an application and certificate of payment\nform as per the contract, which is done after each month end.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nDeferred revenue consists of solar energy system installation\nfees billed to customers for projects which are not completed as of the balance sheet date.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nAllowance for Doubtful Accounts \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe maintain allowances for doubtful accounts for estimated\nlosses resulting from the inability of our customers to make required payments. We estimate anticipated losses based on the expected\ncollectability of all of our accounts receivable, which takes into account collection history, the number of days past due, identification\nof specific customer exposure and current economic trends. When we determine a balance is uncollectible and no longer actively\npursue collection of the account, it is written off.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nInventory \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nInventory consists primarily of solar energy system components\n(such as photovoltaic modules and inverters) located at our warehouses and is stated at the lower of cost (first-in, first-out\nmethod) or market. We identify the inventory items to be written down for obsolescence based on the item\xe2\x80\x99s current sales\nstatus and condition. We write down discontinued or slow-moving inventories based on an estimate of the markdown to retail price\nneeded to sell through our current stock level of the inventories on a quarterly basis.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n\n    33\n    \xc2\xa0\n    \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWarranties\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe warrant solar energy systems sold to customers for up to\nten years against defects in installation workmanship. The manufacturers\xe2\x80\x99 warranties on the solar energy system components,\nwhich are typically passed through to the customers, typically have product warranty periods of 10 years and a limited performance\nwarranty period of 25 years. We generally provide for the estimated cost of warranties at the time the related revenue is recognized.\nWe also maintain specific warranty liabilities for large commercial customers included in discontinued operations. We assess the\naccrued warranty reserve regularly and adjust the amounts as necessary based on actual experience and changes in future estimates.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nCertain prior year amounts have been reclassified for consistency\nwith the current period presentation. These reclassifications had no effect on the reported results of operations. During the\nsecond quarter of 2017, we concluded that it was appropriate to classify items in the statement of operations to conform with\noperating metrics reported to investors and the manner in which management evaluates financial performance and to classify warranty\nliability separately as current and non-current liabilities. Accordingly, we revised the classification of certain items to report\nitems in the statement of operations and balance sheet. These changes in classification did not change the previously reported\noperating income (loss) in the statement of operations, or cash generated (used) from operations in the statement of cash flows,\nor operating income (loss) for any business segment.\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nGoodwill and Purchased Intangibles \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe review goodwill and indefinite-lived intangible assets for\nimpairment during the second quarter, or more frequently if a triggering event occurs between impairment testing dates.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nIntangible assets arising from business combinations, such as\nacquired customer contracts and relationships (collectively \xe2\x80\x9ccustomer relationships\xe2\x80\x9d), are initially recorded at fair\nvalue. Goodwill represents the excess of the purchase price over the fair value of the net identifiable assets acquired in a business\ncombination. Goodwill and purchased intangibles are reviewed for impairment annually or more frequently if impairment indicators\narise.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nUp-front POWERHOUSE\xe2\x84\xa2 3.0 license payments when incurred,\ncosts to obtain UL Certification and legal costs to acquire the license and protect the underlying patents are initially capitalized\nand thereafter amortized to operations, upon achieving UL Certification, on a straight-line basis over the expected life of the\nlicense through 2034.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nDerivative Liabilities\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe account for complex financial instruments including convertible\nnotes, convertible preferred stock, and warrants under ASC 815 and ASC 480.\xc2\xa0 We utilize third party appraisers to determine\nthe fair value of derivative liabilities embedded in complex financial instruments. For financial instruments accounted for as\nliabilities, we defer and amortize to operations costs incurred including the initial fair value of warrants issued and derivative\nliabilities. The issuance\xc2\xa0costs of financial instruments accounted for as equity are charged to additional paid in capital.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nThe extinguishment of financial instruments accounted for as\ndebt that are extinguished by issuance of common stock are recorded at the fair value of common stock issued at the date of issuance,\nwith any difference from the carrying value of the liability recorded as a loss on debt extinguishment.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nShare-Based Compensation \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe recognize compensation expense for share-based awards based\non the estimated fair value of the award on the date of grant. We measure compensation cost at the grant date fair value of the\naward and recognize compensation expense based on the probable attainment of a specified performance condition for performance\nbased awards or over a service period for time based awards. We use the Black-Scholes option valuation model to estimate the fair\nvalue for purposes of accounting and disclosures. In estimating this fair value, certain assumptions are used (see Note\xc2\xa08.\nShare-Based Compensation in Item 8 of this Annual Report), including the expected life of the option, risk-free interest rate,\ndividend yield, volatility and forfeiture rate. The use of different estimates for any one of these assumptions could have a material\nimpact on the amount of reported compensation expense.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n    34\n    \xc2\xa0\n    \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nIncome Taxes \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nWe recognize income taxes under the asset and liability method.\nDeferred income taxes are recognized based on temporary differences between financial reporting and income tax bases of assets\nand liabilities, using current enacted income tax rates and regulations. These differences will result in taxable income or deductions\nin future years when the reported amount of the asset or liability is recovered or settled, respectively. Considerable judgment\nis required in determining when these events may occur and whether recovery of an asset, including the utilization of a net operating\nloss carry-forward prior to its expiration, is more likely than not.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nResults of Operations \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nYear Ended December\xc2\xa031, 2017 Compared to Year Ended\nDecember\xc2\xa031, 2016 \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nContract revenue:\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nSale and installation of solar\nenergy systems. Sale and installation of solar energy system revenue decreased $2.7 million, or 16.1%, to $14 million\nduring the twelve months ended December 31, 2017, from $16.7 million during the twelve months ended December 31, 2016. During\nthe twelve months ended December 31, 2017, installations decreased 0.4 megawatts to 3.7 as compared to the 4.1 megawatts\ninstalled during the twelve months ended December 31, 2016. This decrease was primarily due to slower sales in the beginning\nof the year and a weighted average 10% decline in our ASP. Additionally, the Rhode Island Renewal Energy Growth program\nreached its funding cap earlier than expected through April 1, 2018 which caused a delay in installations originally planned\nfor the fourth quarter of 2017. These projects are projected to be installed in the second quarter of 2018.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nService. Service revenue increased $0.4 million,\nor 53.5%, to $1.1 million during the twelve months ended December 31, 2017 from $0.7 million during the twelve months ended December\n31, 2016. The increase is primarily due to a contract entered into with a third-party in the 4th quarter of 2016 to\nprovide service work on their existing customers.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nContract expenses:\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nInstallation of solar energy systems. Installation\nof solar energy system expenses decreased $1.2 million, or 8.2%, to $13.2 million during the twelve months ended December 31,\n2017, from $14.4 million during the twelve months ended December 31, 2016, which corresponds to the reduction of installation\nrevenue during this same time comparison. We utilize gross margin percentage to measure performance utilizing an internal time\nreporting system allowing us to measure both total incurred contract expense and contract expense excluding construction crew\nidle time. \xc2\xa0For the twelve months ended December 31, 2017, our residential segments gross margin without idle time declined\ndue to a 10% decline in the average selling price per watt exceeding the 2% decline in the costs of installation per watt.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nService. Service expense increased $0.5 million\nto $1.6 million, or 34.8% during the twelve months ended December 31, 2017, from $1.2 million during the twelve months ended December\n31, 2017. The increase is primarily due to the additional costs attributed to a contract entered into with a third-party in the\nfourth quarter of 2016 to provide service work on their existing customers.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nCustomer acquisition. Customer acquisition expense\nincreased $0.1 million during the twelve months ended December 31, 2017, or 2.2%, to $5.9 million from $5.8 million during the\ntwelve months ended December 31, 2016. This increase is primarily attributable to our revenue growth strategy beginning during\nthe first quarter of 2017 resulting in increased hiring in our sales department, an increase in in digital lead development and\nan overall commitment to increased marketing efforts to reduce our cost of acquisition.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nOperating expense. Operating expenses increased $3 million,\nor 39.9%, to $10.7 million during the twelve months ended December 31, 2017 compared to $7.7 million during the twelve months ended\nDecember 31, 2016, primarily due to an increase in labor.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nLitigation and proxy contest expense. Litigation and proxy\ncontest expenses during the twelve months ended December 31, 2017 was $1.5 million compared to $0.02 million during the twelve\nmonths ended December 31, 2016, primarily attributable to proxy contest expenses of $1.2 million in 2017. Our legal expenses may\nincrease in subsequent periods. See Note 5. Commitments and Contingencies.\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n    35\n    \xc2\xa0\n    \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nInterest expense. As a result, there was effectively no interest expense  incurred during the twelve\nmonths ended December 31, 2017, resulting in a $2.8 million decrease from the twelve months ended December 31, 2016.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nDerivative and other. No assurances can be given that should a balance remain on the 2018\nNotes at maturity that we will be successful in meeting the obligation under the 2018 Notes. See Note 6. Other than\nthe uncertainties regarding our ability to obtain additional funding, there are currently no known trends, demands, commitments,\nevents or uncertainties that are likely to materially affect our liquidity.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n201 Tariff \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nBeginning in the second quarter of 2017, certain U.S.\nbased solar module manufacturers  filed a petition under Section 201 of the Trade Act of 1974 (the \xe2\x80\x9c201 Tariff\xe2\x80\x9d)\nclaiming damages as a result of foreign manufacturers exporting lower priced photovoltaic modules into the U.S.\nand recommended the cost of foreign manufactured modules have a floor cost of $0.78 per watt, to be reduced over a\nfour-year period. Currently, we pay a weighted average cost less than the potential floor cost. On September 22, 2017, the\nU.S. International Trade Commission (\xe2\x80\x9cITC\xe2\x80\x9d) decided that the domestic industry had suffered serious harm due\nto imports. After additional public hearings, on October 31, 2017 the ITC provided remedy recommendations to the President\nof the United States which stated (i) that a tariff of 30% to 35% of the solar module cost should be applied and (ii) that\nany such tariff not apply to certain North American treaty partners such as Canada. Approximately 90% of the equipment\nwe purchase is from North American manufacturers.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nAs we sell systems months in advance of their installation and coupled\nwith our cash management approach to adhere to just-in-time procurement of materials, we determined there was sufficient exposure\nto installation margins to warrant bridge buying inventory at our current pricing and procuring panels in excess of current needs\ninto 2018. Accordingly, we have invested in our panel inventory, and as of December 31, 2017, have sufficient panel inventory for\nsubstantially all planned installations during the first quarter of 2018.\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nOn January 22, 2018, the Office of the President of the United\nStates approved in substantial form, recommendations by the ITC to impose a tariff of 30% on imports of solar cells and\nmodules under Section 201 of the Trade Act of 1974, unless specifically excluded. Further, the provisions of the 201 Tariff\nare applicable to imported solar cells and modules from Canada, despite its being a member of the North American Free Trade\nAgreement. We have entered into a bi-annual purchase arrangement with  our North American panel provider to stabilize the\nprice per watt for panels. Accordingly, we do not believe the impact of the 201 Tariff will have a material effect on our\noperations.\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n\n    37\n    \xc2\xa0\n    \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nCash Flows\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nThe following table summarizes our primary sources (uses) of\ncash during the periods presented:\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n    \xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n    2017\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n    2016\xc2\xa0\n\n    Net cash (used in) provided by:\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\n    Operating activities - continuing operations\xc2\xa0\n    $(16,533)\xc2\xa0\n    $(11,313)\n\n    Operating activities - discontinued operations\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa0498\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa0(981)\n\n    Operating activities\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa0(16,035)\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa0(12,294)\n\n    Investing activities\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa0(1,959)\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa0(50)\n\n    Financing activities\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa016,224\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n    \xc2\xa014,690\xc2\xa0\n\n    Net increase (decrease) in cash\xc2\xa0\n    $(1,770)\xc2\xa0\n    $2,346\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nContinuing Operations \n\n\xc2\xa0\n\nOperating activities. tf.Tensor(b'ITEM\xc2\xa07.\xc2\xa0MANAGEMENT\xc2\x92S\xc2\xa0DISCUSSION\xc2\xa0AND\xc2\xa0ANALYSIS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0FINANCIAL\xc2\xa0CONDITION\xc2\xa0AND\xc2\xa0\nRESULTS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0OPERATIONS.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n \xc2\xa0\n Item\xc2\xa07. Management\xc2\x92s Discussion and Analysis of Financial\nCondition and Results of Operations.  MANAGEMENT OVERVIEW \n\xc2\xa0 Introduction \nPier 1 Imports, Inc. is the original global importer of home d\xc3\xa9cor and furniture. The Company directly imports merchandise from many countries, and sells a\nwide variety of decorative accessories, furniture, candles, housewares, gifts and seasonal products in its stores and through the Company\xc2\x92s website, Pier1.com. As of February\xc2\xa027, 2016, the Company operated 1,032 stores in the United States\nand Canada. The following discussion and analysis of financial condition, results of operations, and liquidity and capital resources should be read in conjunction with the accompanying audited Consolidated Financial Statements and notes thereto,\nwhich can be found in Item\xc2\xa08 of this report.  Over the past several years, the Company has transformed from a brick-and-mortar retailer to an\nomni-channel retailer, with the objective of seamless integration across stores, desktop and mobile devices. As part of its transformation to an omni-channel retailer the Company re-launched its e-Commerce capabilities including its website,\nPier1.com, during fiscal 2013. The Company\xc2\x92s focus through the \xc2\x911 Pier 1\xc2\x92 omni-channel strategy is to ensure that customers have an extraordinary experience, regardless of how they shop. By enabling the customer to interact with the\nbrand both in-store and online, the Company expects to maximize selling opportunities, extend brand reach and capture greater market share. The \xc2\x911 Pier 1\xc2\x92 strategy required investment in systems, distribution and fulfillment centers, call\ncenters, distribution network and store development, including new in-store selling tools such as swatch stations, computers and tablets. This strategy also includes a continuing commitment to return excess capital to shareholders through share\nrepurchases and cash dividends.  Fiscal 2015 capped a multi-year period of heavy investment for the Company in support of its transformation to an\nomni-channel retailer, including investment in systems, distribution and fulfillment centers, call centers, distribution network and store development. In fiscal 2016, the Company moderated capital expenditures by over one-third to $51.8 million,\nfrom $81.9 million in fiscal 2015. During fiscal 2016 capital expenditures were deployed toward infrastructure and technology development, including the customer data excellence initiative, supply chain upgrades, existing store improvements and 17\nnew store openings. Capital expenditures in fiscal 2017 are expected to be approximately $55 million to support ongoing investments in technology, stores and distribution centers.  Fiscal 2016 net sales increased 0.4% from the prior year and company comparable sales increased 0.7%. The increases were primarily attributable to the increase in e-Commerce sales, specifically increases in online\ntraffic, online conversion and average ticket compared to last year. During fiscal 2016, e-Commerce sales accounted for 16.1% of net sales compared to 11.1% in the previous fiscal year. E-Commerce sales were the primary driver of total sales growth\nin fiscal 2016, and the Company expects this trend to continue in fiscal 2017. A significant portion of e-Commerce sales touch the retail stores, either by originating on in-store PCs and tablets, or through in-store pick-up. As e-Commerce sales\nhave grown, and to the extent they continue to grow, delivery and fulfillment net costs have also increased and are expected to continue to increase. The Company began to leverage these costs as a percentage of fulfilled sales in fiscal 2016.\n Gross profit for fiscal 2016 was $705.0 million, or 37.3% of sales, compared to $768.5 million, or 40.8% of sales, in the same period last year, a\ndecline of 350 basis points. Merchandise margin (the result of adding back delivery and fulfillment net costs and store occupancy costs to gross profit \xc2\x97 see \xc2\x93Reconciliation of Non-GAAP Financial Measures\xc2\x94) was $1.046 billion\nfor fiscal 2016, or 55.3% of sales, compared to $1.100 billion, or 58.4% of sales for fiscal 2015. The year-over-year decline in merchandise margin as a percentage of sales was primarily attributable to promotional and clearance activity and\ninventory-related inefficiencies within the Company\xc2\x92s distribution center network. Store occupancy costs during fiscal 2016 were leveraged slightly at 15.7% of sales, compared to 15.8% during fiscal 2015. \nOperating income for fiscal 2016 was 4.0% of sales, compared to 6.8% in fiscal 2015. Fiscal 2016 EBITDA (earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and\namortization \xc2\x97 see \xc2\x93Reconciliation of Non-GAAP Financial Measures\xc2\x94) was $125.2 million compared to $176.3 million in fiscal 2015. Net income for fiscal 2016 was $39.6 million, or $0.46 per diluted share, compared to $75.2\nmillion, or $0.82 per diluted share for fiscal 2015.  As the Company\xc2\x92s transition to an omni-channel retailer continues to mature, strategies and\nplans have been initiated and enhanced to drive meaningful top-line sales growth, restore merchandise margin and reduce costs across the organization. These include, but are not limited to: improving merchandise assortments; enhancing marketing\nprograms; optimizing the real \n \xc2\xa0 20\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0PIER 1 IMPORTS, INC.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc3\xaf\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa02016 Form 10-K \n\n\n\n\nTable of Contents\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0ITEM\xc2\xa07.\xc2\xa0MANAGEMENT\xc2\x92S\xc2\xa0DISCUSSION\xc2\xa0AND\xc2\xa0ANALYSIS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0FINANCIAL\xc2\xa0CONDITION\xc2\xa0\nAND\xc2\xa0RESULTS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0OPERATIONS.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0 \xc2\xa0\n \nestate portfolio; reducing store and administrative expenses; improving supply chain efficiencies; managing inventory levels; improving promotional effectiveness; and moderating capital\nexpenditures. In the near-term, profitability will be challenged by a planned increase in media spending, expected continuing promotional and clearance activity and the cost of recent distribution center network inefficiencies. \nThe Company has set out several key guideposts by which to measure the Company\xc2\x92s performance in achieving its objectives, which are: \n\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n1.\nBrand traffic, conversion and average ticket;  \xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n2.\nStores as sales and customer experience centers;  \xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n3.\nMerchandise margin and gross profit;  \xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n4.\nFulfillment and home delivery;  \xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n5.\nSelling, general and administrative expenses; and  \xc2\xa0\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n6.\nCapital allocation.  The Company closed 33 stores, on a net basis,\nduring fiscal 2016. These closures are consistent with, and a part of, the real estate optimization plan previously announced by the Company. The real estate optimization plan includes three parts: (1)\xc2\xa0closure of approximately 100 stores over a\nthree to four fiscal-year period which commenced in fiscal 2016, primarily through natural lease expirations and relocations; (2)\xc2\xa0a more modest new store opening and relocation program; and (3)\xc2\xa0ongoing renegotiations of rent commitments.\n The Company entered into a $200 million Term Loan Facility. As of February\xc2\xa027, 2016, the Company had $197.0 million outstanding under its $200\nmillion Term Loan Facility. Subsequent to year end, through April\xc2\xa020, 2016, under the April 2014 program, the Company utilized a total of $0.8\xc2\xa0million to\nrepurchase 120,000 shares of the Company\xc2\x92s common stock at a weighted average price per share of $7.01 and $46.3\xc2\xa0million remained available for further repurchases under that program. On April\xc2\xa013, 2016, subsequent to year end, the\nCompany announced a $0.07 per share quarterly cash dividend on the Company\xc2\x92s outstanding shares of common stock to shareholders of record on April\xc2\xa027, 2016, which is payable on May\xc2\xa011, 2016. \nOverview of Business  The Company has\nrevised the presentation of the reporting of credit and debit card fees (\xc2\x93Credit Card Fees\xc2\x94) for all periods presented. The Company previously reported Credit Card Fees as a reduction to net sales and has revised its presentation to report\nCredit Card Fees as a component of selling, general and administrative (\xc2\x93SG&A\xc2\x94) expenses. This revised presentation results in an immaterial increase to both net sales and SG&A expenses. There is no impact to operating income, net\nincome, the balance sheet or statement of cash flows. \xc2\xa0\n Company Comparable Sales Calculation\xc2\xa0\xc2\x97\xc2\xa0For fiscal 2016 and fiscal 2015, the company\ncomparable sales calculation included sales that were fulfilled, ordered or sold in a store, provided that the store was open prior to the beginning of the preceding fiscal year and was still open at period end. In addition, orders placed online as\ndirect-to-customer sales (as defined below) were included in the calculation, as a result of direct-to-customer sales being active prior to the beginning of the preceding fiscal year. Remodeled or relocated stores are included if they meet specific\ncriteria. Those criteria include the following: the new store is within a specified distance serving the same market, no significant change in store size and no significant overlap or gap between the store closing and reopening. Such stores are\nincluded in the company comparable sales calculation in the first full month after the reopening. If a relocated or remodeled store does not meet the above criteria, it is excluded from the calculation until it meets the Company\xc2\x92s established\ndefinition as described above.  For the fiscal 2014 company comparable sales calculation, sales included in the calculation were determined in the same\nmanner as above, except that direct-to-customer sales were excluded because those sales did not meet the criteria for inclusion at that time. Net sales during fiscal 2016 were $1.892 billion, an increase of 0.4%, from $1.885 billion in the prior fiscal year. Company comparable sales increased 0.7% for the year which was primarily the result of an\nincrease in online traffic, online conversion and average ticket compared to the prior year. The Company\xc2\x92s e-Commerce sales accounted for 16.1% of net sales for fiscal 2016 compared to 11.1% for fiscal 2015. E-Commerce sales are comprised of\nboth customer orders placed online which were shipped directly to the customer (\xc2\x93direct-to-customer\xc2\x94) and those picked up by the customer at a store location (\xc2\x93store pick-up\xc2\x94). \nSales at the Company\xc2\x92s Canadian stores are subject to fluctuations in currency conversion rates. The year-over-year decline in the value of the Canadian\nDollar, relative to the U.S.\xc2\xa0Dollar, negatively impacted net sales and company comparable sales by approximately 100 basis points in fiscal 2016. Sales on the Pier 1 rewards credit card comprised 34.2% of U.S. sales for the twelve months ended\nFebruary\xc2\xa027, 2016, compared to 32.4% last year. The Company\xc2\x92s proprietary credit card program provides both economic and strategic benefits to the Company. At the end of fiscal 2016, there were 72 of these locations in Mexico and one location in El Salvador. These locations are excluded from the table above. \nMerchandise Margin and Gross Profit  Gross\nprofit for fiscal 2016 was $705.0 million, or 37.3% of sales, compared to $768.5 million, or 40.8% of sales, in the same period last year, a decline of 350 basis points. Merchandise margin (the result of adding back delivery and fulfillment net\ncosts and store occupancy costs to gross profit \xc2\x97 see \xc2\x93Reconciliation of Non-GAAP Financial Measures\xc2\x94) was $1.046 billion for fiscal 2016, or 55.3% of sales, compared to $1.100 billion, or 58.4% of sales, for fiscal 2015. The\nyear-over-year decline in merchandise margin as a percentage of sales was primarily attributable to promotional and clearance activity and inventory-related inefficiencies within the Company\xc2\x92s distribution center network. Delivery and\nfulfillment net costs for fiscal 2016 were $42.5 million, or 2.2% of sales, compared to $32.9 million, or 1.7% of sales, in fiscal 2015. The increase reflects the strong growth of e-Commerce. Store occupancy costs during fiscal 2016 leveraged\nslightly to 15.7% of sales, compared to 15.8% of sales, during fiscal 2015.  SG&A Expenses, Depreciation and Operating Income\n SG&A expenses were $578.8 million in fiscal 2016, compared to $594.9 million in fiscal 2015, a decrease of $16.1 million. As a percentage of\nsales, SG&A expenses were 30.6% in fiscal 2016, compared to 31.6% in fiscal 2015. Depreciation for fiscal 2016 was $50.9 million, compared to $46.3 million in fiscal 2015. This increase was primarily the result\nof additional capital expenditures in recent fiscal years.  In fiscal 2016, the Company recorded operating income of $75.2 million, or 4.0% of sales,\ncompared to $127.3 million, or 6.8% of sales, for fiscal 2015.  Nonoperating Income and Expense \nNonoperating expense for fiscal 2016 was $12.0 million, compared to $6.9 million in fiscal 2015. This increase was primarily the result of interest and related\nexpenses for borrowings on the Term Loan Facility. In the prior year, the Company also recognized gains on the settlement of life insurance policies. \n \xc2\xa0 PIER 1\nIMPORTS, INC.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc3\xaf\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa02016 Form\n10-K\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa023 \n\n\n\n\nTable of Contents\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0ITEM\xc2\xa07.\xc2\xa0MANAGEMENT\xc2\x92S\xc2\xa0DISCUSSION\xc2\xa0AND\xc2\xa0ANALYSIS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0FINANCIAL\xc2\xa0CONDITION\xc2\xa0\nAND\xc2\xa0RESULTS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0OPERATIONS.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n \xc2\xa0\n Income Taxes  The income tax provision for fiscal 2016 was $23.5 million, compared to $45.2 million in fiscal 2015. The decrease in the income tax provision from fiscal 2015 is primarily the result of the Company\xc2\x92s lower\nincome before taxes in fiscal 2016. The effective tax rate for fiscal 2016 was 37.3% compared to an effective tax rate of 37.6% for fiscal 2015. The decrease in the effective tax rate was primarily related to certain favorable discrete items that\noccurred in fiscal 2016, partially offset by certain non-recurring favorable permanent differences and other discrete items that occurred in fiscal 2015.  Net Income and EBITDA  Net income in fiscal 2016 was $39.6 million, or $0.46 per diluted share,\ncompared to $75.2 million, or $0.82 per diluted share, for fiscal 2015. In fiscal 2015, non-GAAP adjusted net income excluding the after-tax effect of retirement related expenses for the Company\xc2\x92s former chief financial officer was $77.2\nmillion, or $0.84 per diluted share. EBITDA for fiscal 2016 was $125.2 million compared to $176.3 million in fiscal 2015. 2015\n(1)\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\n984\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\n81\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\n1,065\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\n\n\n(1)\xc2\xa0\n The Company supplies merchandise and licenses the Pier 1 Imports name to Grupo Sanborns, which sells Pier 1 Imports merchandise primarily in a \xc2\x93store\nwithin a store\xc2\x94 format. At the end of fiscal 2015, there were 68 of these locations in Mexico and one in El Salvador. These locations are excluded from the table above. \nMerchandise Margin and Gross Profit  Gross\nprofit for fiscal 2015 was $768.5 million, or 40.8% of sales, compared to $765.3 million, or 42.7% of sales, in fiscal 2014, a decline of 190 basis points. Merchandise margin (the result of adding back delivery and fulfillment net costs and store\noccupancy costs to gross profit \xc2\x97 see \xc2\x93Reconciliation of Non-GAAP Financial Measures\xc2\x94) was $1.100 billion for fiscal 2015, or 58.4% of sales, compared to $1.068 billion, or 59.6%, for fiscal 2014. The decline in merchandise\nmargin as a percentage of sales was primarily attributable to increased promotional activity in the first half of fiscal 2015 and incremental unplanned supply chain expenses during the fourth quarter of fiscal 2015 primarily related to the\ndistribution centers, which resulted from higher than normal inventory levels. Beginning in the third quarter of fiscal 2015 the Company refined its promotional strategy, utilizing a more targeted approach to promotions, with reduced frequency and\ndepth of all-Company coupons and a more balanced mix between full price and promotional selling. Store occupancy costs during fiscal 2015 were leveraged at 15.8% of sales, compared to 16.1% during fiscal 2014. \nSG&A Expenses, Depreciation and Operating Income  SG&A expenses were $594.9 million in fiscal 2015, compared to $550.9 million in fiscal 2014, an increase of $44.0 million. As a percentage of sales, SG&A expenses were 31.6% in fiscal 2015, compared to\n30.8% in fiscal 2014. Depreciation for fiscal 2015 was $46.3 million compared to $38.9 million in fiscal 2014. This increase was primarily the result of additional capital expenditures in recent fiscal years coupled with incremental\nexpenditures deployed towards technology, which typically depreciate over a shorter time period compared to other depreciable assets, and store closures. \nIn fiscal 2015, the Company recorded operating income of $127.3 million, or 6.8% of sales, compared to $175.5 million, or 9.8% of sales, for fiscal 2014.\n\n \xc2\xa0 PIER 1\nIMPORTS, INC.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc3\xaf\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa02016 Form\n10-K\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa025 \n\n\n\n\nTable of Contents\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0ITEM\xc2\xa07.\xc2\xa0MANAGEMENT\xc2\x92S\xc2\xa0DISCUSSION\xc2\xa0AND\xc2\xa0ANALYSIS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0FINANCIAL\xc2\xa0CONDITION\xc2\xa0\nAND\xc2\xa0RESULTS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0OPERATIONS.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n \xc2\xa0\n Nonoperating Income and Expense  Nonoperating expense for fiscal 2015 was $6.9 million, compared to $0.9 million in fiscal 2014. This increase was primarily the result of interest on borrowings under the Term Loan Facility and related expenses of\napproximately $8.4 million, partially offset by gains on the settlement of life insurance policies.  Income Taxes \nThe Company had an effective tax rate of 37.6% and recorded income tax expense of $45.2 million in fiscal 2015 compared to an effective tax rate of 38.4% and income\ntax expense of $67.1 million in fiscal 2014. The decrease in the effective tax rate was primarily due to certain non-recurring favorable permanent differences and other discrete items occurring during fiscal 2015. The decrease in income tax expense\ncompared to fiscal 2014 was primarily due to the Company\xc2\x92s lower income before income taxes in fiscal 2015.  Net Income and\nEBITDA  Net income in fiscal 2015 was $75.2 million, or $0.82 per diluted share, compared to $107.5 million, or $1.01 per diluted share, for fiscal\n2014. In fiscal 2015, non-GAAP adjusted net income excluding the after-tax effect of retirement related expenses for the Company\xc2\x92s former chief financial officer was $77.2 million, or $0.84 per diluted share. EBITDA for fiscal 2015 was $176.3\nmillion compared to $215.4 million in fiscal 2014. See \xc2\x93Reconciliation of Non-GAAP Financial Measures\xc2\x94 below.  RECONCILIATION OF\nNON-GAAP FINANCIAL MEASURES  \xc2\xa0 The Company\nreports its financial results in accordance with U.S. generally accepted accounting principles (GAAP). This Annual Report on Form 10-K references non-GAAP financial measures including merchandise margin, EBITDA, adjusted net income, adjusted diluted\nearnings per share and contribution from operations.  The Company believes the non-GAAP financial measures referenced in this Annual Report on Form 10-K\nallow management and investors to understand and compare results in a more consistent manner for the fiscal years ended February\xc2\xa027, 2016,\xc2\xa0February\xc2\xa028, 2015 and March\xc2\xa01, 2014. Non-GAAP financial measures should be considered\nsupplemental and not a substitute for the Company\xc2\x92s results reported in accordance with GAAP for the periods presented.  In fiscal 2015, net income\nincluded retirement related expenses for the Company\xc2\x92s former chief financial officer. Contribution from operations represents gross profit, less compensation for operations (which includes store and customer service payroll) and operational expenses. EBITDA represents earnings before interest, taxes,\ndepreciation and amortization. Management believes merchandise margin, contribution from operations and EBITDA are meaningful indicators of the Company\xc2\x92s performance which provide useful information to investors regarding its financial\ncondition and results of operations. Management uses merchandise margin, contribution from operations and EBITDA, together with financial measures prepared in accordance with GAAP, to assess the Company\xc2\x92s operating performance, to enhance its\nunderstanding of core operating performance and to compare the Company\xc2\x92s operating performance to other retailers. These non-GAAP financial measures should not be considered in isolation or used as an alternative to GAAP financial measures and\ndo not purport to be an alternative to net income or gross profit as a measure of operating performance. LIQUIDITY AND CAPITAL RESOURCES \n\xc2\xa0 The Company\xc2\x92s cash and cash equivalents totaled $115.2\nmillion at the end of fiscal 2016, an increase of $15.2 million from the fiscal 2015 year-end balance. The increase was primarily due to cash provided by operating activities of $164.0 million, partially offset by the utilization of cash to fund the\nCompany\xc2\x92s capital investments and to return excess capital to shareholders, including $51.8 million for capital expenditures, $75.0 million to repurchase shares of the Company\xc2\x92s common stock under the April 2014 program and $23.7 million\nfor cash dividends.  The Company\xc2\x92s cash and cash equivalents totaled $100.1 million at the end of fiscal 2015, a decrease of $26.6 million from the\nfiscal 2014 year-end balance of $126.7 million. The decrease was primarily the result of the utilization of cash to support the Company\xc2\x92s growth plan and return of excess capital to shareholders, including $81.9 million for capital\nexpenditures, $185.5 million to repurchase shares of the Company\xc2\x92s common stock under the board approved share repurchase programs and cash dividends of $21.6 million. These expenditures were partially offset by cash provided by operating\nactivities of $65.7 million and net proceeds of $198.0 million from the closing of the Term Loan Facility.  Cash Flows from Operating\nActivities  Operating activities provided $164.0 million of cash in fiscal 2016, primarily as a result of net income adjusted for non-cash items as\nwell as a decrease in inventory. Inventory levels at the end of fiscal 2016 were $405.9 million, a decrease of $73.0 million, or 15%, from the end of fiscal 2015. The increase in cash flows from operating activities for fiscal 2016 compared to\nfiscal 2015 is due to favorable changes in cash flows primarily related to inventories.  Operating activities provided $65.7 million of cash in fiscal\n2015, primarily as a result of $75.2 million of net income and a $26.3 million increase in accounts payable and other liabilities, primarily due to increased purchases of merchandise, offset by a $101.2\n\n \xc2\xa0 PIER 1\nIMPORTS, INC.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc3\xaf\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa02016 Form\n10-K\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa027 \n\n\n\n\nTable of Contents\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0ITEM\xc2\xa07.\xc2\xa0MANAGEMENT\xc2\x92S\xc2\xa0DISCUSSION\xc2\xa0AND\xc2\xa0ANALYSIS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0FINANCIAL\xc2\xa0CONDITION\xc2\xa0\nAND\xc2\xa0RESULTS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0OPERATIONS.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n \xc2\xa0\n \nmillion increase in inventory. The increase in inventory over fiscal 2014 was primarily due to increased purchases during fiscal 2015 in anticipation of higher forecasted sales including\nincreased inventory for the Company\xc2\x92s special order program and additional purchases made due to the shift in the timing of Chinese New Year.  Cash Flows from Investing Activities  During fiscal 2016, the Company\xc2\x92s investing\nactivities used $51.7 million, compared to $83.3 million during fiscal 2015. Total capital expenditures in fiscal 2016 were $51.8 million, which were deployed toward infrastructure and technology development, supply chain upgrades, existing store\nimprovements and new store openings. The Company expects total capital expenditures to be approximately $55 million in fiscal 2017 to support ongoing investments in technology, stores and distribution centers. \nDuring fiscal 2015, the Company\xc2\x92s investing activities used $83.3 million, compared to $70.2 million during fiscal 2014. Total capital expenditures in fiscal\n2015 were $81.9 million, which were deployed toward the opening of 30 new stores, new merchandise fixtures for existing stores, other leasehold improvements, and technology and infrastructure initiatives, including enhancements to the omni-channel\nplatform. The Company also invested in its distribution network and completed its second fulfillment center in Columbus, Ohio.  Cash\nFlows from Financing Activities  Financing activities for fiscal 2016 used $97.2 million, primarily resulting from cash outflows of $75.0 million for\nrepurchases of the Company\xc2\x92s common stock pursuant to the April 2014 program and the payment of quarterly cash dividends of $0.07 per share per quarter for each quarter of fiscal 2016, totaling $23.7 million. See \xc2\x93Share Repurchase\nProgram\xc2\x94 below for more information.  Financing activities for fiscal 2015 used $9.0 million of cash, primarily related to cash outflows of\n$185.5 million for repurchases of the Company\xc2\x92s common stock pursuant to the April 2014 program and the share repurchase program approved by the Board in October of 2013, which included $11.6 million for shares repurchased in fiscal 2014 that\nsettled in fiscal 2015, and the payment of quarterly cash dividends of $0.06 per share per quarter for each quarter of fiscal 2015, totaling $21.6 million. This utilization of cash was offset by $198.0 million of net proceeds from borrowings under\nthe Term Loan Facility. See \xc2\x93Revolving Credit Facility,\xc2\x94 \xc2\x93Term Loan Facility\xc2\x94 and \xc2\x93Share Repurchase Program\xc2\x94 below for more information.  Revolving Credit Facility  The Company completed a second amendment to its Revolving Credit\nFacility in April of 2014, in order to allow additional borrowings under the Term Loan Facility that closed at the same time. The Revolving Credit Facility is secured primarily by merchandise inventory and third-party credit card receivables and\ncertain related assets on a first priority basis and is secured on a second lien basis by substantially all other assets of certain of the Company\xc2\x92s subsidiaries, subject to certain exceptions. Credit extensions under the Revolving Credit\nFacility are limited to the lesser of $350.0 million or the amount of the calculated borrowing base, which was $341.4 million as of February\xc2\xa027, 2016. Under the Revolving Credit Facility, the Company had no cash borrowings and $37.6 million in\nletters of credit and bankers\xc2\x92 acceptances outstanding, with $303.8 million remaining available for cash borrowings, all as of February\xc2\xa027, 2016.  Term Loan Facility  The Term Loan Facility matures on April\xc2\xa030, 2021, and is secured by a\nsecond lien on all assets subject to a first lien under the Revolving Credit Facility and a first lien on substantially all other assets of certain of the Company\xc2\x92s subsidiaries, subject to certain exceptions. At the Company\xc2\x92s option,\nborrowings under the Term Loan Facility will bear interest, payable quarterly or, if earlier, at the end of each interest period, at either (a)\xc2\xa0the LIBOR rate (as defined in the Term Loan Facility) subject to a 1% floor plus 350 basis points\nper year or (b)\xc2\xa0the base rate (as defined in the Term Loan Facility) subject to a 2% floor plus 250 basis points per year. As of February\xc2\xa027, 2016, the Company had $197.0 million outstanding under the Term Loan Facility with a carrying\nvalue of $192.9 million, net of unamortized discounts and debt issuance costs. The proceeds of the loan were used for general corporate purposes, including working capital needs, capital expenditures, and share repurchases and dividends permitted\nunder the Term Loan Facility. The Term Loan Facility is subject to quarterly amortization of principal equal to 0.25% of the original aggregate principal amount of the loans, with the balance due at final maturity. The Company is subject to an\nannual excess cash flow repayment requirement, as defined in the facility. At the Company\xc2\x92s option, and subject to the requirements and provisions of the Term Loan Facility, the Company can prepay borrowings under the Term Loan Facility at any\ntime. The fair value of the Term Loan Facility was approximately $188.1 million as of February\xc2\xa027, 2016, which was measured at fair value using the quoted market price. The Term Loan Facility was classified as Level 2 based on the frequency and\nvolume of trading for which the price \n \xc2\xa0 28\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0PIER 1 IMPORTS, INC.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc3\xaf\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa02016 Form 10-K \n\n\n\n\nTable of Contents\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0ITEM\xc2\xa07.\xc2\xa0MANAGEMENT\xc2\x92S\xc2\xa0DISCUSSION\xc2\xa0AND\xc2\xa0ANALYSIS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0FINANCIAL\xc2\xa0CONDITION\xc2\xa0\nAND\xc2\xa0RESULTS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0OPERATIONS.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0 \xc2\xa0\n \nwas readily available. Level 2 inputs include quoted prices in active markets for similar assets or liabilities; quoted prices for identical or similar assets or liabilities in markets that are\nnot active; or other inputs that are observable or can be corroborated by observable market data for substantially the full term of the asset or liability.  The Term Loan Facility includes restrictions on the Company\xc2\x92s ability to, among other things, incur or guarantee additional indebtedness, pay dividends on, or redeem or repurchase shares of the Company\xc2\x92s\ncapital stock, make certain acquisitions or investments, materially change the business of the Company, incur or permit to exist certain liens, enter into transactions with affiliates or sell the Company\xc2\x92s assets to, or merge or consolidate\nwith or into, another company, in each case subject to certain exceptions. The Term Loan Facility does not require the Company to comply with any financial maintenance covenants, but contains certain customary representations and warranties,\naffirmative covenants and provisions relating to events of default. The Term Loan Facility provides for incremental facilities, subject to certain conditions, including the meeting of certain leverage ratio requirements as defined therein, to the\nextent such facilities exceed an incremental $200.0 million.  Share Repurchase Program \nDuring fiscal 2016, the Company repurchased approximately 8% of the Company\xc2\x92s common stock outstanding at the beginning of the year under the April 2014\nprogram. As of February\xc2\xa027, 2016, the Company had repurchased 12,669,435 shares of its common stock under the April 2014 program at a weighted average cost of $12.06 per share for a total cost of $152.8 million, and $47.2 million remained\navailable for further repurchases. In fiscal 2016, the Company had cash outflows of $75.0 million related to shares of common stock repurchased in fiscal 2016. Subsequent to year end, through April 20, 2016, under the April 2014 program, the Company\nutilized a total of $0.8 million to repurchase 120,000 shares of the Company\xc2\x92s common stock at a weighted average price per share of $7.01 and $46.3 million remained available for further repurchases under that program. \nDuring fiscal 2015, the Company repurchased approximately 10% of the Company\xc2\x92s common stock outstanding at the beginning of fiscal 2015 under the share\nrepurchase program announced in October of 2013 and the April 2014 program. The Company\xc2\x92s share repurchase program announced on October\xc2\xa018, 2013, was completed on April\xc2\xa010, 2014 with total repurchases during fiscal 2015 of 5,071,812\nshares at a weighted average cost of $18.95 per share for a total cost of $96.1 million. The April 2014 program was announced on April\xc2\xa010, 2014. As of February\xc2\xa028, 2015, the Company had repurchased 5,208,500 shares of its common stock\nunder the April 2014 program at a weighted average cost of $14.94 per share for a total cost of $77.8 million, and $122.2 million remained available for further repurchases. In fiscal 2015, the Company had cash outflows of $185.5 million related to\nshare repurchases. These share repurchases included $173.9 million for shares of common stock repurchased in fiscal 2015 and $11.6 million for shares of common stock repurchased in fiscal 2014 that settled in fiscal 2015. \nDividends Payable  On April\xc2\xa013, 2016,\nsubsequent to year end, the Company announced a $0.07 per share quarterly cash dividend on the Company\xc2\x92s outstanding shares of common stock. The\nCompany has an umbrella trust, currently consisting of five sub-trusts, which was established for the purpose of setting aside funds to be used to settle certain benefit plan obligations. Two of the sub-trusts are restricted to satisfy obligations\nto certain participants in the Company\xc2\x92s supplemental retirement plans. The assets of the two sub-trusts consisted of interest bearing investments of less than $0.1 million at both February\xc2\xa027, 2016 and February\xc2\xa028, 2015, and were\nincluded in other noncurrent assets. The remaining three sub-trusts are restricted to meet the funding requirements of the Company\xc2\x92s non-qualified deferred compensation plans. These trusts\xc2\x92 assets are included in other noncurrent assets\nand are comprised of investments and life insurance policies. The investments totaled $9.9 million and $10.6 million at February\xc2\xa027, 2016 and February\xc2\xa028, 2015, respectively. The investments were held primarily in mutual funds and are\nstated at fair value. These sub-trusts also own and are the beneficiaries of life insurance policies on the lives of former key executives. These policies are stated at fair value. The cash surrender value of the policies was approximately $5.9\nmillion and $5.7 million as of February\xc2\xa027, 2016 and February\xc2\xa028, 2015, respectively, and the death benefit was approximately $11.4 million and $11.3 million, respectively.  In addition, the Company owns and is the beneficiary of a number of insurance policies on the lives of former key executives that were unrestricted as to use at the end of fiscal 2016. The cash surrender value of\nthe unrestricted policies was approximately $13.4 million and $13.1 million at February\xc2\xa027, 2016 and February\xc2\xa028, 2015, respectively, and was included in other noncurrent assets. These policies had a death benefit of approximately $20.1\nmillion and $19.9 million as of February\xc2\xa027, 2016 and February\xc2\xa028, 2015, respectively. At the discretion of the Company\xc2\x92s Board, contributions of cash or unrestricted life insurance policies may be made to one or more of the\nsub-trusts. The Company has a variety of sources for liquidity, which include available cash\nbalances and borrowings against the Company\xc2\x92s Revolving Credit Facility and Term Loan Facility. The Company\xc2\x92s current plans for fiscal 2017 include a capital expenditure plan slightly higher than fiscal 2016 and continuation of cash\ndividends and repurchases of the Company\xc2\x92s common stock. The Company does not presently anticipate any other significant cash outflows in fiscal 2017 other than those discussed herein or those occurring in the normal course of business.\n\n \xc2\xa0 30\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0PIER 1 IMPORTS, INC.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc3\xaf\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa02016 Form 10-K \n\n\n\n\nTable of Contents\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\n\xc2\xa0\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0ITEM\xc2\xa07.\xc2\xa0MANAGEMENT\xc2\x92S\xc2\xa0DISCUSSION\xc2\xa0AND\xc2\xa0ANALYSIS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0FINANCIAL\xc2\xa0CONDITION\xc2\xa0\nAND\xc2\xa0RESULTS\xc2\xa0OF\xc2\xa0OPERATIONS.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0 \xc2\xa0\n The Company\xc2\x92s key drivers of cash flows are sales, management of inventory levels, vendor payment terms,\nmanagement of expenses and capital expenditures.
